  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_405982ed-57d8-4e19-b8f2-b7dd33bc95ce'
      });
    }

  <span id="freeText9433658584358887660" style="display:none">"Stepping Heavenward" is the fictional coming of age story of a young Christian girl named Katherine. The story follows her life from when she is sixteen, though courtship, engagement, marriage, having children, and the many challenges that she confronts in her adult life. This classic Christian story is told through a series of journal entries by Katherine and is an inspirational tale for young girls who themselves are facing the very same challenges of growing up.</span>

        });

(yes all authors inevitably put in biography in their fiction but that aside) This is why I loathed the book, for I started it thinking it was one woman&#39;s spiritual journey, only to see it was not.     <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1777300093" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1777300093" />
    <a title="Zachary McIntire" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53081511-zachary-mcintire"><img alt="Zachary McIntire" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1488167940p2/53081511.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1777300093?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zachary McIntire" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zachary McIntire" href="/user/show/53081511-zachary-mcintire">Zachary McIntire</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53081511-zachary-mcintire?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1777300093" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12466571901646193880">Since I'm apparently the first guy to read this book in recent recorded history, I feel somewhat obligated to try and give it a thorough review. Unfortunately, the fact that I ended up reading it in two parts, over the space about five months, makes that somewhat difficult. Still, I will do my best.<br/><br/>At times, especially in the early going, this approached a four-star read for me. I love character studies, especially ones in historical settings, and since this was of course written at a time conte</span>
  <span id="freeText12466571901646193880" style="display:none">Since I'm apparently the first guy to read this book in recent recorded history, I feel somewhat obligated to try and give it a thorough review. Unfortunately, the fact that I ended up reading it in two parts, over the space about five months, makes that somewhat difficult. Still, I will do my best.<br /><br />At times, especially in the early going, this approached a four-star read for me. I love character studies, especially ones in historical settings, and since this was of course written at a time contemporary with its setting, I'm assuming it can be relied upon to give a fairly accurate picture of the life and perspectives of New-England Christians in the mid-nineteenth century. Its value in this connection is significant, and is a large part of what held my interest through about two-thirds of the book. <br /><br />One of the most fascinating things to me about the historical element was how a woman of the time would not refer to pregnancy at all, not even in her private journal. The appearance of her babies on the scene was always a complete surprise to me, and sometimes it took a bit for me to figure out what she was talking about, even after they were born!<br /><br />As others have noted, the latter third of the book gets to be a little tiresome, that being the reason I left off reading it for about four months. In my view, the book could have, and should have been shorter: there was a great deal of repetition, which probably added to the reader's experience of Katy's life (she keeps going over and over the same ground, spiritually) but it makes it rather difficult to get through in places. <br /><br />My main reason for picking up this book, actually, is that the author, Elizabeth Payson Prentiss, also penned <em>More Love to Thee O Christ</em>, a hymn of consecration that is an enduring favorite of mine. From this fact, and from the title, I expected sanctification to be a major topic, and this indeed proved to be the case. Sometimes, I felt the author handled the subject fairly well, but in the main, I was slightly out of harmony with her perspectives.<br /><br />It seems to me that, if this book is a faithful representation of the practical theology of New England Christians in the Victorian era, then theirs was often a pretty bleak and dismal faith. While it's true that God uses suffering to refine us, I don't believe this is the only tool in His box, so to speak. Nor do I believe, as seems to be presented, that it is only possible to achieve truly Christlike character through a life spent in silent submission to extremely trying circumstances.<br /><br />The other major perspective that I couldn't really agree with, was the related idea that sanctification is, and should be expected to be, very gradual, coming as a result suffering, and of our efforts at maintaining spiritual disciplines. Sometimes, when the protagonist went to her spiritual mentors for advice, I got the feeling that that advice amounted to "keep on trying," which is a perfect recipe for failure and frustration. What I felt was missing from the theology of these characters, was an understanding of the person and work of the Holy Spirit. It is not up to us to sanctify ourselves, only to yield to God's work in our lives, to be emptied of ourselves, and filled with Christ. I felt that if the main character had been led to grasp this truth, she could have avoided a great deal of the Romans-7-style wrangling she seemed to be engaged in for much of the book.<br /><br />On the positive side, this book does contain some beautiful portraits of submission, made even more meaningful by the knowledge that the author's life was at least as tragic as her main character's, and perhaps even more so. The fact that she could express such sentiments as those contained in this book, and in her best-known hymn, testifies to me of the work of the Spirit in her life, even if she wasn't fully conscious of it.<br /><br />Even though, by the end, I was more than ready for the book to be over, I'm not sorry I commenced to read it. I do not think it really deserves the "ladies-only" reputation it seems to have acquired; readers of either gender could enjoy it, provided they have an appreciation for character study and theological reflection, and don't mind wading through the slow parts. (Oh, and provided they can stand reading about how it took a couple about the first ten years of their marriage to figure out they should, like, <em>talk</em> to each other and stuff. Who knew?)<br /><br />One final note: the free version of this book on Kindle (or at least, the one I got) has a <em>lot</em> of typos, which can make for choppy reading. I would recommend getting a library copy, or buying a better Kindle version.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12466571901646193880" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer823660" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3961374402186833577">a fictional diary from the point of view of a Christian teenage girl who is hot-tempered and overanalytical (sounds like me!)... the story follows her as she truly dedicates her life to Christ, although she grew up in a believing family, and as she goes through life. first love, marriage, family and more... loved this book.<br/><br/>brothers... it's not just a book for girls. if ur interested in figuring out a little of the way the mind of a gal works, please read it. she's so honest with her feelings and</span>
  <span id="freeText3961374402186833577" style="display:none">a fictional diary from the point of view of a Christian teenage girl who is hot-tempered and overanalytical (sounds like me!)... the story follows her as she truly dedicates her life to Christ, although she grew up in a believing family, and as she goes through life. first love, marriage, family and more... loved this book.<br /><br />brothers... it's not just a book for girls. if ur interested in figuring out a little of the way the mind of a gal works, please read it. she's so honest with her feelings and the way her temperament swiftly changes according to small actions by her husband. i loved seeing how marriage sanctifies a person... not that i'm ready! but now i get it. =)<br /><br />read it.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1779097266?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lydia Howe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lydia Howe" href="/user/show/35587655-lydia-howe">Lydia Howe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35587655-lydia-howe?shelf=read-in-2016">read-in-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35587655-lydia-howe?shelf=2016-three-star">2016-three-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35587655-lydia-howe?shelf=2016-fiction">2016-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1779097266" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13244996163572453842">I read this book a long time ago, then decided to re-read it after Kate (<a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://onceuponanordinary.wordpress.com'>https://onceuponanordinary.wordpress.com</a>) mentioned it during a recent blogging party.<br/><br/>Overall I think this book has some amazing points. I especially like the first half, or maybe two thirds. The main character is someone who I relate to on a lot of levels (although I don't get quite as horrified with myself as she does). She learns a lot of good lessons during the books, and therefore it's practical. <br/><br/>Unfortunately, the boo</span>
  <span id="freeText13244996163572453842" style="display:none">I read this book a long time ago, then decided to re-read it after Kate (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://onceuponanordinary.wordpress.com">https://onceuponanordinary.wordpress.com</a>) mentioned it during a recent blogging party.<br /><br />Overall I think this book has some amazing points. I especially like the first half, or maybe two thirds. The main character is someone who I relate to on a lot of levels (although I don't get quite as horrified with myself as she does). She learns a lot of good lessons during the books, and therefore it's practical. <br /><br />Unfortunately, the book has a habit of dragging on. And on. And on. The story is written as a journal, and there are times when six or seven years are skipped at once. To me that kind of ruins the flow, and I'd have liked it much more if the book would have ended much sooner than it did. I ended up skimming a lot of the last 30 pages or so. <br /><br />The book also contains some very "good" examples of how important it is to communicate, because the characters don't communicate. at. all. And it drives me nuts. It causes so much stress when people don't talk and let miscommunications fester.<br /><br />I was also reading an e-version of the book, and there were tons of weird spellings, typos, and strange characters thrown in there, so I didn't actually get the full book, because some sentences I simply couldn't read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13244996163572453842" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2785008862978613705" style="display:none">Good news: If you are looking for a free audiobook, Theresa L. Downey did a fantastic job! It's one of the better Librivox recordings I've listened to. <br /><br />Bad News: I wanted to love this book so much. I stuck it out to the half-way point but then... I was just so mad. Katie has a lot of faults I loved how realistically that was portrayed. However, when she is hurt or frustrated, everyone just said that she has a problem. The last straw came when her father-in-law says she is to attached to her infant son.<br />I appreciate the realistic way this book was written, but I wished Katie's husband would have stood up for her instead of telling her his sister would do a better job of running things anyway. Or that her mother and pastor would have given her words in encouragement as well as admonishment.<br /><br />I might try this book again, after all, there is a lot in here to admire. But I'm setting it aside for now. </span>

Main character Kate tells the raw details of heartache, despair, and fear as well as her joys as she seeks to devote her whole self to Christ.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1612125393188478559">Written in 1869, this is a fictional journal of a young woman--her beginning, doubts, disobedience, growth, marriage, difficult in-laws, children, and her older days--and is still pertinent to women today.<br/><br/>It's good to read a book (the second time) in which the main character, at least in the main, listens to and learns from her mother and other older and wiser people. The main character has a lot of flaws, yet she wants to please God, and slowly grows. I can relate to many of her struggles, so I</span>
  <span id="freeText1612125393188478559" style="display:none">Written in 1869, this is a fictional journal of a young woman--her beginning, doubts, disobedience, growth, marriage, difficult in-laws, children, and her older days--and is still pertinent to women today.<br /><br />It's good to read a book (the second time) in which the main character, at least in the main, listens to and learns from her mother and other older and wiser people. The main character has a lot of flaws, yet she wants to please God, and slowly grows. I can relate to many of her struggles, so I find it interesting and helpful. No perfect books can be found, except the Bible in its original language, but I still think this is a book worthy of five stars, or maybe four and a half.<br /><br />The author, Elizabeth Prentiss, also penned the famous hymn, "More Love to Thee, O Christ."</span>

<div id="review_1522853060" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1522853060" />
    <a title="Tori Samar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5886759-tori-samar"><img alt="Tori Samar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598639956p2/5886759.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1522853060?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tori Samar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tori Samar" href="/user/show/5886759-tori-samar">Tori Samar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5886759-tori-samar?shelf=christian">christian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5886759-tori-samar?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5886759-tori-samar?shelf=women">women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5886759-tori-samar?shelf=for-christian-women">for-christian-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5886759-tori-samar?shelf=christian-living-and-growth">christian-living-and-growth</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5886759-tori-samar?shelf=nineteenth-century">nineteenth-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1522853060" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16006862045847890680">How refreshing it is to read a work of Christian fiction that's not simply masquerading as a "Christian" romance novel! Here we have a book that sets out to show the spiritual journey of one woman as she deals with various people and events (often tragic ones) in her life. It's a fictional depiction of someone's Christian worldview growing and developing and coming to bear on every moment of life. I certainly wish I could find more books like these in the Christian fiction realm. <br/><br/>As much as I li</span>
  <span id="freeText16006862045847890680" style="display:none">How refreshing it is to read a work of Christian fiction that's not simply masquerading as a "Christian" romance novel! Here we have a book that sets out to show the spiritual journey of one woman as she deals with various people and events (often tragic ones) in her life. It's a fictional depiction of someone's Christian worldview growing and developing and coming to bear on every moment of life. I certainly wish I could find more books like these in the Christian fiction realm. <br /><br />As much as I liked most of the plot points and spiritual points in the book, I do think the journal format sometimes hindered the emotional impact of the story because it forced the author to do a lot of telling instead of showing. Some of the most tragic events in the story didn't grab me nearly as much as they could/should have because I as the reader had to hear about rather than see them. The author also resorted to some large time gaps that I felt negatively affected the story's pacing.<br /><br />My one other criticism of the book is that it presents a weak view of reading novels and various other works of fiction (an incredibly ironic fact since <i>Stepping Heavenward</i> is itself technically fiction). Because the view is tucked in among many excellent spiritual truths, some may read this book and find themselves adopting the same attitude toward fiction reading. Some context first: many in the 19th century (the time period in which this book was written), including many Christians, held to the view that novel/fiction reading was dangerous, a waste of time, and/or good only as recreation (this view continues to exist today in certain circles, including Christian ones). Once the main character in this book is converted to Christianity, she comes to adopt the view that most fiction reading is now a waste of time. What fiction she does keep around she uses only for her times of personal recreation. In other words, the book presents the idea that the more a person grows spiritually, the more they will come to realize that fiction reading is something either to avoid entirely or to do very little of. While I recognize that a lot of garbage fiction was floating around in the 1800s, my problem is that <i>Stepping Heavenward</i> treats all fiction the same way (even Shakespeare doesn't escape!). And I believe that kind of position is misguided. Can fiction be dangerous? Yes, but not always. Can fiction be a waste of time to read? Yes, but not always. Can fiction be good for recreation? Yes, but much of it can (and is meant to!) rise so far above mere entertainment! I have read plenty of fiction that strengthened, not harmed me. I have read plenty of fiction that was absolutely well worth every minute I spent reading it. I have read fiction that shaped me morally, intellectually, and imaginatively for the better. I have read fiction to enjoy it as good, beautiful art that reflects and points to the glory of the great Artist. In fact, I have found that the more I grow spiritually, the more I <i>gain</i> from picking up a good work of fiction. So while <i>Stepping Heavenward</i> does get many things right, I that it gets this one point quite wrong.<br /><br />To reiterate, however, this is still a great book overall! It is a credit to the Christian fiction genre, unlike most of what is published today. I am just very passionate about upholding the value of all good fiction. :-)</span>
  <a data-text-id="16006862045847890680" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_526198590" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/526198590" />
    <a title="Cassie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/434960-cassie"><img alt="Cassie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445981227p2/434960.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/526198590?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cassie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cassie" href="/user/show/434960-cassie">Cassie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer526198590" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4455447610883444661">To me this book has become as challenging as some of the greatest daily devotionals (think Oswald Chambers) and inspired me to journal for myself not just the happy things of my life but my deepest spiritual struggles as well. There are so many ways that I can relate to Katherine's journey and each time I read it I relate to something new. So many treasures of faith to glean from this book! <br/><br/>One of my favorite passages reflecting the struggle of accepting what Christ has offered us so freely - <br/><br/>"'</span>
  <span id="freeText4455447610883444661" style="display:none">To me this book has become as challenging as some of the greatest daily devotionals (think Oswald Chambers) and inspired me to journal for myself not just the happy things of my life but my deepest spiritual struggles as well. There are so many ways that I can relate to Katherine's journey and each time I read it I relate to something new. So many treasures of faith to glean from this book! <br /><br />One of my favorite passages reflecting the struggle of accepting what Christ has offered us so freely - <br /><br />"'Are you miserable now?' She asked.<br />'Yes, just now I am. I do not mean that I have no happiness; I mean that I am in a disheartened mood, weary of going round and round in circles, committing the same sins, uttering the same confessions, and making no advance.'<br />'My dear,' she said after a time, 'have you a perfectly distinct, settled view of what Christ is to the human soul?'<br />'I do not know. I understand, of course, more or less perfectly that my salvation depends on Him alone; it is His gift.'<br />'But do you see with equal clearness that your sanctification must be as fully His gift as your salvation is?"<br />'No,' I said after a thought. 'I have had a feeling that He has done His part and now I must do mine.'<br />'My dear,' she said with as much tenderness and feeling, 'then the first thing you have to do is to learn Christ.'<br />'But how?'<br />'On your knees, my child, on your knees!'"<br /><br />I have finished this book feeling encouraged to deepen my relationship with my Savior and "learn Christ" for myself. <br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1959031324" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1959031324" />
    <a title="Michele Morin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20287479-michele-morin"><img alt="Michele Morin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1500679871p2/20287479.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1959031324?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michele Morin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michele Morin" href="/user/show/20287479-michele-morin">Michele Morin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1959031324" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9696266008376131484">“Write what you know.”<br/>It’s good counsel, and, if followed, results in a kind of authenticity that can’t happen if the author attempts to write outside her realm of real-life experience. Maybe that’s why people are still reading Stepping Heavenward by Elizabeth Prentiss, a fictional journal that follows the life of Katherine Mortimer from her first entry at age 16 [“How dreadfully old I am getting!”] to her final entry just before her death.<br/><br/>Like the author, Katherine lost her father at a young ag</span>
  <span id="freeText9696266008376131484" style="display:none">“Write what you know.”<br />It’s good counsel, and, if followed, results in a kind of authenticity that can’t happen if the author attempts to write outside her realm of real-life experience. Maybe that’s why people are still reading Stepping Heavenward by Elizabeth Prentiss, a fictional journal that follows the life of Katherine Mortimer from her first entry at age 16 [“How dreadfully old I am getting!”] to her final entry just before her death.<br /><br />Like the author, Katherine lost her father at a young age and suffered from a variety of physical ailments. The intersection between fiction and reality becomes even more pronounced as Katherine struggles to allow her suffering to “do its perfect work” in her life. Through weariness and discouragement, through joy and fresh resolve, the message of Stepping Heavenward is ageless and relevant to wives and mothers set in all times (and might just encourage their men-folk, too). Written in 1869, the quaint style and slow pace is charming, and I smiled at the extreme modesty of that era in which babies just appeared in the narrative with only veiled references to pregnancy (and certainly none whatsoever to the delivery!), and I winced at the eagerness of mothers to have their children’s gums lanced to ease teething discomfort [really??] and at the prevalence of infant mortality and debilitating illnesses.<br /><br />These were hard times compared to the 21st century, and yet Elizabeth harnesses Katie’s sufferings and points her readers to a God who “notices the most trivial act, accepts the poorest, most threadbare little service, listens to the coldest, feeblest petition, and gathers up with parental fondness all our fragmentary desires and attempts at good works. Oh, if only we could begin to conceive how much He loves us, what different creatures we should be!”<br /><br />It was heartening to see Katie’s trajectory of growth and to receive her offerings of homely wisdom:<br /><br />“One must either stop reading the Bible altogether, or else leave off spending one’s whole time in just doing easy, pleasant things one likes to do.”<br />(And this was written in the days before binge-watching Netflix was a thing . . .)<br /><br />In an era when women were not encouraged to read deeply or to flex their theological muscles, Elizabeth Prentiss offered solid teaching on various topics, all embedded within the narrative arc of Katie’s life.<br /><br />On the sacred versus secular dichotomy:<br /><br />“You speak of going back to your music as if that implied going away from God. You rush from one extreme to another. The only true way to live in this world, constituted just as we are, is to make all our employments subserve the one great end and aim of existence, namely , to glorify God and to enjoy Him forever.”<br />On mothering:<br /><br />“What a fearful thing it is to be a mother! But I have given my children to God.”<br /><br />“When you speak contemptuously of the vocation of maternity, you dishonor, not only the mother who bore you, but the Lord Jesus Himself, who chose to be born of a woman, and to be ministered unto by her through a helpless infancy.”<br />On perfectionism:<br /><br />“I am a little afraid of ‘good people.’ I fancy that they are always criticizing me and expecting me to imitate their perfection.”<br />On prayer:<br /><br />“I have learned, at least, to face and fight such distractions, instead of running away from them as I used to do. My faith in prayer, my resort to it, becomes more and more the foundation of my life, and I believe . . . that nothing but prayer stands between my soul and the best gifts of God.”<br />On perseverance through trials:<br /><br />“There is no wilderness so dreary but that His love can illuminate it, no desolation so desolate but that He can sweeten it. I know what I am saying. It is no delusion. I believe that the highest, purest happiness is known only to those who have learned Christ in sick-rooms, in poverty, in racking suspense and anxiety, amid hardships, and at the open grave.”<br />If the author’s name, Elizabeth Prentiss, rings a bell, check your nearest hymnal, for in addition to Stepping Heavenward, Elizabeth also wrote “More Love to Thee,” and I will share the lyrics below. You can also click here to see a YouTube video of the hymn sung by Fernando Ortega.<br /><br />More love to Thee, oh Christ, more love to Thee!<br />Hear Thou the prayer I make on bended knee.<br />This is my earnest plea<br />More love, oh Christ, to Thee<br />More love to Thee, more love to Thee!<br />Once earthly joy I craved, sought peace and rest<br />Now Thee alone I seek, give what is best.<br />This all my prayer shall be<br />More love, oh Christ to Thee<br />More love to Thee, more love to Thee!<br />Elizabeth Prentiss wrote with the aim of encouraging others along the path of a fierce discipleship. I’ve been intending to read Stepping Heavenward ever since the days when Elisabeth Elliot was recommending it on her radio program, and now, since it is in public domain, it is available very inexpensively in various editions. The author joins Peter in exhorting her readers to “think it not strange concerning the fiery trial which is to try you, as though some strange thing happened to you.” The struggle to pray, to be patient, and to care for others is very real, but so is the comfort that God brings to the heart that looks to Him for daily strength.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9696266008376131484" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_111247718" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/111247718" />
    <a title="Caroline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3909735-caroline"><img alt="Caroline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585423112p2/3909735.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/111247718?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caroline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caroline" href="/user/show/3909735-caroline">Caroline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Every girl </span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Caroline by: </span>
      Mom
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3909735-caroline?shelf=favorite">favorite</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer111247718" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6953874917584217272">This is probably one of the most excellent, most encouraging books I have ever read! My mom suggested it to me several years ago, but I just recently picked it off the shelf. Stepping Heavenward follows Katherine Eliot’s life from the age of sixteen until her death as she pours out her soul through her journal. Although written in the 1800’s, the issues she deals with (such as doubts about her salvation, an unruly temper, disrespect to parents, death of loved ones, marriage, etc.) are as relevan</span>
  <span id="freeText6953874917584217272" style="display:none">This is probably one of the most excellent, most encouraging books I have ever read! My mom suggested it to me several years ago, but I just recently picked it off the shelf. Stepping Heavenward follows Katherine Eliot’s life from the age of sixteen until her death as she pours out her soul through her journal. Although written in the 1800’s, the issues she deals with (such as doubts about her salvation, an unruly temper, disrespect to parents, death of loved ones, marriage, etc.) are as relevant today as they were then. I have to admit, I was at first afraid this book would be a self-righteous discourse on the specific rules one must follow in order to be a “proper Christian.” But as I began to read, I became thoroughly engrossed in Katy’s honest, heartfelt insight into her vibrant faith. She does not gloss over anything, but presents her life as it is, faults and all. I felt myself at many time throughout the novel thinking, “I struggle with that, too!” Katy’s transparent and relatable way of phrasing her thoughts as she brings her imperfections to Christ completely riveted me. Although not flawless, her precious marriage was especially inspiring. It was such a simple picture of true love in which Jesus is the focus. Throughout the book, her gradual and pure surrender to Christ and His will was so indescribably beautiful and refreshing that at the end I was (yes, I will admit) crying. If you are a girl, no matter what age, this book is one you MUST read. It showed me that other Christian women, even though living centuries ago, have had struggles and thoughts just like mine but always find our Savior faithful. I can certainly see myself twenty years from now snuggling up on the couch and reading it aloud to my own daughter. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6953874917584217272" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14029123345854357219">Wow. I didn't expect to like this one as much as I did. It follows the life of a woman from sixteen on into later adult years. Prentiss is very candid in describing the inner personal struggle that a Christian often faces on their spiritual journey.<br/><br/>If you are a person who tends to use the word preachy in a derogatory manner then this one may not be for you. However, I found it very uplifting. There are lots of little encouraging messages scattered throughout the book, and I thought they were hel</span>
  <span id="freeText14029123345854357219" style="display:none">Wow. I didn't expect to like this one as much as I did. It follows the life of a woman from sixteen on into later adult years. Prentiss is very candid in describing the inner personal struggle that a Christian often faces on their spiritual journey.<br /><br />If you are a person who tends to use the word preachy in a derogatory manner then this one may not be for you. However, I found it very uplifting. There are lots of little encouraging messages scattered throughout the book, and I thought they were helpful and inspiring. <br /><br />I looked at it as sort of a devotional with a gripping life story attached rather than the other way around. I found it hard to put down, and it stayed on my mind when I wasn't reading it. I imagine some of the things I learned will stay with me for quite a while.<br /><br />It's definitely one that I will be re-reading. Possibly every year. <br /><br />By the way, the physical edition is way better than the kindle version. I switched to kindle for a little bit and then switched back because of all the typos and mistakes.</span>

I thought Prentiss was, perhaps, laying it on a little thick.<br /><br />Fast forward almost 3 decades and I found a whole lot more to appreciate! With more of a tested "faith walk" of my own, I appreciate the beauty of faith in hard circumstances which Prentiss is describing throughout this story. I was challenged and inspired this time 'round with no suspicion that Prentiss might not have known what she was talking about. She knew. I get it. And she has called me to rise up and bless the Lord in all things.</span>

  <span id="freeText11994800640447684244" style="display:none">This may be a top 5 book of books I’ve read in my adult years, certainly in the last 5 since becoming a mother. This book kept popping up in different places; my pastor’s wife recommended it, one of the hosts on the Risen Motherhood podcast shared about it, and finally my aunt tucked it into a package she sent when my 4th child was born and said it was one of her favorites. I knew it had to be good, but I don’t know how I’ve made it 32 years without finally getting a copy in my hands! Katy’s character development and her exploration and understanding that our marriages and mothering bring our greatest sanctification (if God so puts us in those roles) was beautifully relatable. Even written in a completely different time period, the content was so easily understood in today’s culture. I already gave a copy to a friend for her birthday last week and plan on including it in upcoming baby shower gifts. A wonderful read! </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4659483317607821184">One of my favorite books. Written as a journal, it follows the spiritual journey of a girl from about 16 years well into motherhood... It challenged my walk with Christ more than anything I've read in a long time. It's worth reading every couple of years. <br/><br/>Some Favorite quotes:<br/><br/>You will imagine that it is best that He should at once enable you to see clearly. If it is, you may be sure He will do it. He never makes mistakes. But he often deals far differently with his discisples. He lets them grope</span>
  <span id="freeText4659483317607821184" style="display:none">One of my favorite books. Written as a journal, it follows the spiritual journey of a girl from about 16 years well into motherhood... It challenged my walk with Christ more than anything I've read in a long time. It's worth reading every couple of years. <br /><br />Some Favorite quotes:<br /><br />You will imagine that it is best that He should at once enable you to see clearly. If it is, you may be sure He will do it. He never makes mistakes. But he often deals far differently with his discisples. He lets them grope their way in the dark until they fully learn how blind they are, how helpless, how absolutely in need of Him.<br /><br />Lay down this principle as law - God does nothing arbitrary. If he takes away your health, for instance, it is because He has some reason for doing so; and this is true of everything you value, and if you have real faith in Him, you will not insist on knowing the reason why.<br /><br />God knows whom He can trust! He would not lay hiw hand thus on all his children!<br /><br />Recreaton we must have. Otherwise the strings of our soul, sound up to an unnatural tension, will break.<br /><br />I think some of the best, most contrite, most useful of men and women, whose prayers prevail with God and bring down blessings into the homes in which they dwell, often possess unlovely traits that furnish them with their best discpline. The very fact that they are ashamed of themselves drives them to God; they feel safe in his presence. And while they lie in the very dust of self-confusion at his feet, they are dear to him and have power with him.<br /><br />4 Steps that Will Lead to Peace:<br />1. Be desirous of doing the will of another, rater than thine own.<br />2. Choose always to have less, rather than more.<br />3. Seek always the lowest place and to be inferior to everyone<br />4. Wish always, and pray, that the will of God may be wholly fulilled in thee<br /><br />Re Children:<br />Here is a sweet, fragrant mouth to kiss; here are two more feet to make music with their pattering about my nursery. Her eis a soul to train for God; and the body in which it dwells is worth all it will cost, since it is the abode of a kingly tenant.<br /><br />The best convent...for a woman is the seclusion of her own home. There she may find her vocation and fight the battles, and there she may learn the reality and the earnestness of life.<br /><br />(Up<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18850.On_Being_Ill" title="On Being Ill by Virginia Woolf" rel="nofollow noopener">on being ill</a> and confined to a bed...)<br />My comfort is in my perfect faith in the goodness and love of my father, my certainty that He had a reason in thus afflicting me that I shoudl admire and adore if I knew what it was. ANd in the midst of my sorrow I have had and do have a delight in Him hitherto unknown, so that sometimes this room in which I am a prisoner seems like hte very gate of heaven.<br /><br />When you speak contemptuously of the vocation of maternity, you dishonor not only the mother who bore you, but the Lord Jesus Himself, who chose to be born of woman and to be ministered unto by her through a helpless infancy.<br /><br />(After having buried a child, brother and her mother...)<br />I can give up my child, my only brother, my darling mother without a word; but to receive every tiresome visitor as sent expressly and directly to weary me by the Master himself, to meet every negligence on the part of hte servants as His choice for me at the moment; to be satisfied and patient when [my husband:] gets particularly absorbed in his books, because my Father sees that little discipline suitable for me at the time, all this I have not fully learned.<br /><br />I hve always aimed at this flexibility. I think a mother, especially, ought to learn to enter into the gayer moods of her children at the very moment when her own heart is sad. And it may be as religious an act her her to romp with them at one time as to pray with them at another.</span>

<div id="review_33564621" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33564621" />
    <a title="Mikejencostanzo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/752096-mikejencostanzo"><img alt="Mikejencostanzo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540007427p2/752096.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33564621?book_show_action=true">Sep 22, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mikejencostanzo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mikejencostanzo" href="/user/show/752096-mikejencostanzo">Mikejencostanzo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/752096-mikejencostanzo?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33564621" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2708884959783104773"><u>Stepping Heavenward</u> is the journal of a woman who lived in the 1800's. We follow her life from her first entry as a 16 year old until her last entry shortly before her death. Sometimes, her journal lies unwritten in for months, even years at a time. But always, she returns to it and the entries it contains combine to make a coherent & really quite glorious story when it's all said and done.<br/><br/>I think one of the most wonderful things about following the life of Katy Elliott is how truly genuine and </span>
  <span id="freeText2708884959783104773" style="display:none"><u>Stepping Heavenward</u> is the journal of a woman who lived in the 1800's. We follow her life from her first entry as a 16 year old until her last entry shortly before her death. Sometimes, her journal lies unwritten in for months, even years at a time. But always, she returns to it and the entries it contains combine to make a coherent &amp; really quite glorious story when it's all said and done.<br /><br />I think one of the most wonderful things about following the life of Katy Elliott is how truly genuine and contemporary her story is. Katy, as a young woman in her 19th century garb, somehow melts the borders of time. She struggles with teenage rebellion -- and not just in a quaint, old-fashioned, got-into-Mother's-hair-tonic sort of way. Rather, she reveals true ugliness of heart in her journal entries. Her journey towards conversion and life as a new believer is not picture-perfect. She reveals her struggles honestly with anger, annoyance, pride &amp; gloating. Over the years, she mellows, becoming gentler &amp; more beautiful, but not because of one quick-fix "Aha" moment where everything comes together. Her life instead is utterly authentic.<br /><br />I appreciated the fact too that this was not just a "young woman" story. I get the sense that this book is recommended mainly as inspirational reading for pre-teen girls in sheltered, conservative Christian homes. "Give them something historical to read -- that will be safe." But this book is so much more full-orbed than just a "safe for the young readers" book. It can easily hold its own on the "Reading for Young Moms" booklist, and the "Reading for Older Women Who are Empty Nesters" booklist. It captures womanhood in it's wholeness -- not just a snapshot of one season, but rather the whole picture.<br /><br />Another wonderful thing that I absolutely loved about this book was how clearly &amp; beautifully Katy understood the gospel message of Jesus Christ. I think we often have this picture of "folks in the olden days" as legalistic, moralistic &amp; stodgy. Katy's understanding of liberty &amp; freedom in Christ is so remarkably fresh that it could have come straight out of the letters of the apostle Paul. Katy, was used mightily by God to preach the good news of freedom from legalism to her father-in-law on his deathbed -- what a beautiful reminder that the gospel is completely timeless!<br /><br />My journey through <u>Stepping Heavenward</u> was one I will always remember. I will always have a special place in my heart for the character of Katy Elliott. I will always remember that little twinge of disappointment at finding that she wasn't a real person. Believe it or not, I read the entire book, under the false impression that I was reading an auto-biography. Imagine my surprise when I learned that it was a work of fiction! Well done &amp; kudos to author Elizabeth Prentiss, also a native of the 1800's. Although the character who sprung from your imagination was sadly not a true historical figure, your voice has still crossed the centuries and engaged a reader like me.<br /><br />--Jen</span>

    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    }

      if (form) {

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_11281852'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11281852-kisses-from-katie?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Kisses from Katie<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/4875266.Katie_Davis?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Katie      Davis<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.41 avg rating &mdash; 27,747 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2011\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer12792432648360504743\">What would cause an eighteen-year-old old senior class president and homecoming queen from Nashville, Tennessee, to disobey and disappoint her parents by forgoing college, break her little brother’s heart, lose all but a handful of her friends (becau<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText12792432648360504743\" style=\"display:none\">What would cause an eighteen-year-old old senior class president and homecoming queen from Nashville, Tennessee, to disobey and disappoint her parents by forgoing college, break her little brother’s heart, lose all but a handful of her friends (because the rest of them think she has gone off the deep end), and break up with the love of her life, all so she could move to Uganda, where she knew only one person but didn’t know any of the language? A passion to make a difference. Katie Davis left over Christmas break her senior year for a short mission trip to Uganda and her life was turned completely inside out. She found herself so moved, so broken by the people and the children of Uganda that she knew her calling was to return and care for them. Her story is like Mother Teresa’s in that she has given up everything—at such a young age—to care for the less fortunate of this world. Katie, a charismatic and articulate young woman, has gone on to adopt 14 children during her time in Uganda, and she completely trusts God for daily provision for her and her family, which includes children with special needs. \n\nTo further her reach into the needs of Ugandans, Katie established Amazima Ministries. The ministry matches orphaned children with sponors worldwide. Each sponsor\'s \$300/year provides schooling, school supplies, three hot meals a day, minor medical care, and spiritual encouragement. Katie expected to have forty children in the program; she had signed up 150 by January 2008; today it sponsors over 400. Another aspect of the ministry is a feeding program created for the displaced Karamojong people—Uganda\'s poorest citizens. The program feeds lunch to over 1200 children Monday-Friday and sends them home with a plate for food; it also offers basic medical care, Bible study, and general health training.\n\nKatie Davis, now The woman who aspires to raise children for the glory of God, to develop the ministry of the home, or to co-labor with her husband is deemed “old-fashioned” or “unfulfilled.” The modern lure of independence and career has bewitched an entire generation to exchange the beauty of Christian womanhood for the temporary enticements of a society at war with the family.\n\nAmidst this confusion, Kathleen Norris’s Mother is a refreshing call to sanity. Mother is the fictional tale of a young lady who leaves home and repudiates family life in the hope of finding personal fulfillment through independence and a career. She decides that home life is a poor choice in the face of life in the big city. But God dramatically changes her heart as she realizes that wealth and position are illusory and that independence can enslave a young lady. She discovers that the greatest woman she has ever known is her mother and now she longs for home and motherhood.\n\nAfter reading this book, editor Jennie Chancey wrote, “That night, I opened the book, intending to read a chapter or two before bed. An hour passed before I realized how far I had read. I could not put the book down and finished it just shortly before midnight. As I turned the last page, tears filled my eyes. I knelt by my bed and asked God to forgive me for my bitterness and my unwillingness to trust and obey Him.”\n\nVision Forum is pleased to offer this restored and revised version of the 1911 classic She once considered herself unmotivated by the lure of prosperity, but upon being called “rich” by an undeniably poor child, evidence to the contrary mounted, and a social experiment turned spiritual was born.7 is the true story of how Jen (along with her husband and her children to varying degrees) took seven months, identified seven areas of excess, and made seven simple choices to fight back against the modern-day diseases of greed, materialism, and overindulgence.Food. Clothes. Spending. Media. Possessions. Waste. Stress. They would spend thirty days on each topic, boiling it down to the number seven. Only eat seven foods, wear seven articles of clothing, and spend money in seven places. Eliminate use of seven media types, give away seven things each day for one month, adopt seven green habits, and observe “seven sacred pauses.” So, what’s the payoff from living a deeply reduced life? What did Jesus mean to say to us? What is his will for us today? Drawing on the Sermon on the Mount, Dietrich Bonhoeffer answers these timeless questions by providing a seminal reading of the dichotomy between &quot;cheap grace&quot; and &quot;costly grace.&quot; &quot;Cheap grace,&quot; Bonhoeffer wrote, &quot;is the grace we bestow on ourselves...grace without discipleship....Costly grace is the gospel which must be sought again and again, the girl which must be asked for, the door at which a man must know....It is costly because it costs a man his life, and it is grace because it gives a man the only true life.&quot;The Cost of Discipleship is a compelling statement of the demands of sacrifice and ethical consistency from a man whose life and thought were exemplary articulations of a new type of leadership inspired by the Gospel, and imbued The<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText428466430747585990\" style=\"display:none\">In 1956, five young men, including Elliot\'s husband, Jim, traveled into the jungles of Ecuador to establish communication with the fierce Huaorani Tribe, a people whose only previous response to the outside world has been to attack all strangers. The men\'s mission combined modern technology with innate ingenuity, sparked by a passionate determination to get the gospel to those without Christ. In a nearby village, their wives waited to hear from them. The news they received - all five missionaries had been murdered - changed lives around the world forever. Written while she was still a missionary in South America and at the request of the men\'s families, Through Gates of Splendor was Elisabeth Elliot\'s personal account of the final mission of these five courageous men. Filled with quotations from letters, material from personal journals, a wealth of photographs, and an epilogue update, this reprint of the original hardcover edition tells a lasting story of God\'s grace, unconditional love, and great courage. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6665692835583449455">ELIZABETH PRENTISS (1818 -1878) was the daughter of an early nineteenth-century revival preacher and began writing as a teenager. Born in 1818 in Portland, Maine, Prentiss was also the writer of the hymn "More Love to Thee, O Christ." Prentiss died in Vermont in 1878.</span>
